**Mechanisms considered:** `const BANNER: &str` (built-in default or user `include_str!("banner.txt")`) — the rust-native 'on the classpath' = embed at compile time, no runtime resource loading; thin macro splitting the banner template's placeholders at MACRO TIME into a const segment list (Literal | Placeholder{key}) — like value-injection R1's grammar split; malformed grammar is a compile error at the macro span; hand-writable print step: `match banner_mode { Off => return, Console => write_stdout(render(segments, env)?).await, Log => tracing::info!(target:"leaf.banner", "{}", render(segments, env)?) }`; render() walks const segments calling env.resolve_placeholders only on placeholder segments; ANSI from a const palette, stripped for Log/non-tty; NO Banner trait, NO BannerSource chain, NO BannerSink — a custom banner is a different const string; the dynamic case is an explicit escape-hatch closure `Application::banner_fn(|env| async move { .. })`; BannerMode tri-state read by the same precedence (default Console < programmatic setter < leaf.main.banner-mode bound from Environment); Log mode routed directly to startup-instrumentation's tracing target (`target: "leaf.banner"`), no bespoke sink; rendered-banner-as-singleton ceremony questioned and likely DROPPED (a §2.2 judgement) unless a downstream consumer needs it; thin macros emit nothing at #[leaf::main]; only banner_text!/#[banner] does the placeholder split — fully hand-writable, cargo expand legible

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** This is the cross-crate-INERT realization (the major T1 win, directly paralleling value-injection R1). The banner path consumes only the Environment read view + a const string and registers NO cross-crate descriptor, so it has ZERO DCE/linker exposure (no Layer 0/A/B hazard), needs NO force-link discipline, needs NO anti-DCE self-check, and gets FULL wasm/embedded support — a notable contrast to R1, which drags the fragile discovery substrate into a cosmetic feature. The resolution+render logic (render, the Segment type, BannerError) lives in leaf-boot, named by absolute path so emitted code hard-codes ::leaf_boot::banner::...; the macro itself holds ZERO logic (only the grammar split), in leaf-macros (proc-macro isolation per the cross-crate doc). include_str! is the honest analogue of classpath-relative banner.txt — 'the banner ships embedded in the binary', deterministic, platform-independent, no runtime resource loading and so no coupling to the resource-loading neighbour's eventual decision. The tradeoff: an integration crate CANNOT contribute a banner via discovery (no descriptor chain) — a cross-crate banner must be passed explicitly by the app (banner_text/banner_fn, Architecture D escape-hatch shape), which for banners (app-level cosmetics, never shipped by integration crates) is the right call but is inconsistent with every other contribution's auto-discovery. The mode-knob name (leaf.main.banner-mode) + placeholder vocabulary remain a small stable config surface. Process stdout (Console) is the only global resource, written once at one cold moment. Generics are a non-issue (no descriptor to register). This realization is the one that proves the banner does NOT need to participate in the cross-crate discovery machinery at all.

**T2 — execution context:** Cold-path single-shot on the bootstrap task, even leaner than R1. Mode resolution + source selection are fully synchronous (a const string is already in hand; no chain walk). render() is synchronous CPU-only (walk const segments, call env.resolve_placeholders which is an in-memory walk per property-resolution's sync-non-blocking contract, apply/strip ANSI) — NOTHING awaits in render. The only `.await` is the final write: Console writes process stdout (an async writer, or spawn_blocking around a synchronous stdout write if it would block on a full pipe — documented §2.3 discipline), Log emits via tracing::info! (synchronous emit into the subscriber, reactive §2.4, no busy-wait). The escape-hatch banner_fn closure is the one place a custom banner MAY await real work, awaited explicitly at the print step. NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5) — stdout written once by the sole bootstrap-task writer at this phase. PARENT LINKAGE: a leaf.banner.print child step under the run root, explicit (no ambient thread-local). CANCELLATION: a cancelled bootstrap mid-banner drops the partial write; nothing to teardown (no async-Drop). ERRORS: BannerError (an unresolved strict placeholder, an IO write error) is a rich Result but degrades to no-banner + a warning by the non-fatal-cosmetic policy; malformed grammar and missing-embedded-file are impossible at runtime (caught at compile time). No singleton-registration cross-thread concern if the ceremony is dropped (R2's default).

#### R3 — Build-time-frozen banner artifact: the banner's STRUCTURE (resolved mode-default, the rendered template with statically-knowable placeholders pre-substituted, ANSI pre-applied, char metrics) is computed once at build time into an inspectable RenderedBanner const, and the run step does only the genuinely-runtime work (resolve runtime placeholders, pick the channel, write)
Push the AOT lineage (the run-pipeline R3's RunPlan instinct) onto the banner specifically: separate the banner's STRUCTURE from its EXECUTION. A PLAN/build phase (build.rs / leaf-codegen, OR a runtime startup-prep pass when not AOT) resolves everything statically knowable about the banner: the default BannerMode (from config defaults + the resolved feature set), the active banner template (built-in default vs user include_str! vs build-known contribution), the placeholders that are STATICALLY KNOWABLE at build time pre-substituted (e.g. ${application.version} and ${application.title} read from Cargo metadata — CARGO_PKG_VERSION/CARGO_PKG_NAME — and ${leaf.version} from leaf's own metadata, all known at build time!), the ANSI styling pre-applied, and the char/line metrics precomputed. It emits a `const RENDERED_BANNER: PartialBanner { pre_rendered_lines: &[&str], runtime_placeholder_slots: &[(usize, &str)], ansi_applied: bool, .. }` checked-in/const artifact (Architecture C, sqlx model). The RUNTIME print step is then a THIN executor consuming the frozen PartialBanner: read the resolved BannerMode (the build-time default overridden by the runtime-bound leaf.main.banner-mode — a runtime override of a planned value, the one genuinely-dynamic knob), if Off return, else fill ONLY the runtime_placeholder_slots (placeholders that depend on runtime Environment values — rare for a banner; most are app/leaf version + title, all build-time-known) by calling env.resolve_placeholders on those keys, then write the assembled lines to the mode-selected channel (Console stdout / Log tracing target). The KEY OBSERVATION specific to the banner: nearly ALL banner placeholders (application.version, application.title, leaf.version) are KNOWN AT BUILD TIME (they are Cargo/leaf metadata, not runtime config) — so the banner is the feature where build-time pre-rendering is MOST complete (unlike a general RunPlan where much is runtime). Validation moves earliest: a malformed template or an unresolvable build-time placeholder fails the BUILD with zero runtime side effects. The same thin executor serves AOT-emitted and runtime-computed PartialBanners (no bimodality). The frozen PartialBanner is INSPECTABLE (a generated const a user can read) — 'what will my banner literally look like' is a build artifact, not emergent runtime behaviour.

**Pros:** Best fit for the §3 compile-vs-runtime keystone applied to a feature where it ACTUALLY pays off maximally: a banner's placeholders (application.version, application.title, leaf.version) are nearly all CARGO/leaf METADATA known at build time, so the banner can be pre-rendered ~completely at build time — far more than a general RunPlan can. The runtime work shrinks to 'maybe fill a runtime slot, pick the channel, write'.; Zero runtime placeholder-resolution cost in the common case (overhead #2): the version/title/leaf-version substitutions happen at build time, so steady-state startup just writes a pre-rendered const — even leaner than R2's runtime render() walk for the (dominant) all-build-time-placeholders case.; Earliest + richest fail-fast (charter §1.7): a malformed banner template or an unresolvable build-time placeholder is a BUILD error with complete context and zero runtime side effects — strictly earlier than R1's runtime BannerError or even R2's macro-span error (which catches grammar but not build-time-resolvable keys).; The frozen PartialBanner is INSPECTABLE data (a generated const / dumped artifact): 'what does my banner literally render to' is a readable build artifact, not behaviour you must run the app to see — excellent reproducibility/debuggability (sqlx --check lineage), and the thin-executor story keeps it §2.10-legible.; Architecture C (build-time codegen, the cross-crate doc's most portable path) sidesteps ALL DCE layers AND is fully platform-independent incl. wasm/embedded — the banner artifact is emitted deterministically from Cargo metadata, so a build-known banner contribution is cross-checked at build time (--check-able) rather than via a fragile runtime registry.; Identical thin executor across AOT-emitted and runtime-computed PartialBanners means ONE banner code path serves the native-image/optimized build and the dev build — no bimodality, swappable substrate per target (the cross-crate doc's identical-runtime-API goal).

**Cons:** Heaviest machinery for the SMALLEST feature — a whole PartialBanner IR + a build-time pre-render phase + a runtime slot-fill executor is wildly disproportionate to 'print a string', and pre-judges the §3 compile-vs-runtime AND metadata/codegen keystones HARDEST for a cosmetic step. It must stay strictly a CANDIDATE; presenting build-time-frozen banner rendering as THE realization would bias phase 2's signal far out of proportion to the feature's importance.; The mode (console/log/off) can FLIP at runtime from bound config (leaf.main.banner-mode), and a runtime banner.txt at an operator-supplied path (Spring's spring.banner.location pointing at an external file) is inherently runtime — so the plan needs a runtime override/fallback for exactly the cases Spring's banner most flexibly supports, partially reintroducing the runtime path it sought to freeze (the same bindToApplication-flips-the-plan tension as run-pipeline R3, here for the mode and the source).; An operator-supplied EXTERNAL banner file (resolved at runtime by path) has NO build-time presence — it forces a runtime-load lane that erodes the frozen-artifact shape, so R3 is clean ONLY for the embedded/default-banner case and degrades to R1/R2's runtime resolution for the (real, if rare) external-file case.; Two computation modes (build-time codegen + runtime-computed PartialBanner) that must produce IDENTICAL banners, plus the runtime fallback for external/dynamic banners, is essentially R1/R2's path plus a build step — so the payoff (build-time pre-render of metadata placeholders) is bounded and the coherence burden (same banner from both modes) is real for a feature whose entire output is decorative.; A build.rs / codegen step for a BANNER is a notable build-time tax and 'less magic' cost for near-zero functional benefit over R2's include_str! + const-segment approach, which already resolves the build-time-known placeholders cheaply (CARGO_PKG_VERSION is available to a macro via env! too) WITHOUT a full artifact IR.; The PartialBanner artifact becomes a stable format/ABI commitment that must version cleanly across leaf releases — an absurd amount of stability surface to commit for a startup banner, the clearest case of the realization's machinery dwarfing its value.

**Mechanisms considered:** build-time PLAN phase (build.rs / leaf-codegen, or a runtime startup-prep pass when not AOT) pre-rendering everything statically knowable about the banner WITHOUT runtime side effects; a frozen `const RENDERED_BANNER: PartialBanner { pre_rendered_lines, runtime_placeholder_slots, ansi_applied, char_metrics }` artifact (Architecture C, sqlx model); build-time pre-substitution of metadata placeholders: ${application.version}=CARGO_PKG_VERSION, ${application.title}=CARGO_PKG_NAME, ${leaf.version}=leaf metadata — all KNOWN AT BUILD TIME (the banner's distinctive property); thin runtime executor: read mode (build-time default overridden by runtime-bound leaf.main.banner-mode) → if Off return → fill only runtime_placeholder_slots via env.resolve_placeholders → write assembled lines to the mode-selected channel; build-time validation: malformed template / unresolvable build-time placeholder fails the BUILD with zero runtime side effects (earliest fail-fast); runtime override/fallback lane for the mode flip + an operator-supplied external banner file (spring.banner.location analogue) the plan can't freeze; identical executor across AOT-emitted and runtime-computed PartialBanners (no bimodality); the artifact is --check-able; inspectable generated PartialBanner const (sqlx --check lineage) — 'what my banner renders to' as a build artifact; Architecture C substrate — platform-independent incl. wasm/embedded, sidestepping all DCE layers; Log channel routing identical to R1/R2 (startup-instrumentation tracing target); Console writes process stdout

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The realization that confronts build-time resolution most directly via Architecture C (the cross-crate doc's most-portable, DCE-immune path). A build.rs / cargo leaf prepare reads Cargo metadata (CARGO_PKG_VERSION/NAME, leaf's version) + a banner-source convention and emits the PartialBanner into OUT_DIR, include!'d into the binary — sidestepping Layer 0/A/B entirely and being fully platform-independent incl. wasm. The banner is UNIQUELY suited to this because its placeholders are mostly build-time metadata, not runtime config — so the build-time pre-render is near-complete (unlike a general RunPlan). BUT it cannot freeze an operator-supplied EXTERNAL banner file (no build-time presence) — that needs a runtime-load lane, and the mode can flip at runtime from bound config — so the artifact is clean only for the embedded/default case. The PartialBanner format becomes a stable codegen ABI commitment (grossly disproportionate stability surface for a banner — a key con). leaf-codegen owns the artifact emission; leaf-boot owns the executor. CRITICALLY: even R2 gets the build-time-known placeholders cheaply via env!("CARGO_PKG_VERSION") in the macro WITHOUT a full build.rs artifact — so R3's incremental cross-crate benefit over R2 is small (build-time validation + an inspectable artifact) at a large build-step cost. A build-known banner contribution would be cross-checked at build time (--check-able) rather than via a runtime registry — the one place R3's manifest-cross-check beats R1's runtime discovery, but for a feature where a missing banner is the lowest-stakes failure imaginable. CANDIDATE only, held to keep the compile-vs-runtime signal unbiased.

**T2 — execution context:** The PLAN phase is SYNCHRONOUS build-time work (single-threaded, no runtime IO, before the binary runs or in a dedicated runtime-prep pass) producing the PartialBanner + the earliest (build-time) diagnostics. The runtime EXECUTOR is a thin cold-path step on the bootstrap task: read the resolved mode (synchronous env read for the runtime override), if Off return, fill only the runtime_placeholder_slots (synchronous env.resolve_placeholders — typically zero or one slot since most placeholders were build-time-frozen), then the single `.await` is the write (Console stdout async writer / spawn_blocking, or Log tracing emit — identical to R1/R2). Because the banner is pre-rendered, the runtime CPU work is near-zero (assemble pre-rendered lines + maybe one slot fill). NO GLOBAL LOCK — stdout written once by the bootstrap task. PARENT LINKAGE: a leaf.banner.print child step under the run root, explicit. CANCELLATION: drop the partial write, nothing to teardown. ERRORS: build-time validation failures are compile/prepare errors with complete context; the only runtime errors are a write IO failure or an unresolvable runtime-slot placeholder (degraded to no-banner + warn by the non-fatal-cosmetic policy) plus the runtime external-file-load failure in the fallback lane. The runtime mode-override is a synchronous read reconciled against the planned default before the write — a tiny execution-context boundary where the plan meets runtime reality. The external-banner-file fallback lane awaits file IO on the bootstrap task (cold), the one place the frozen artifact yields to runtime resolution.

### Rust clashes
- No classpath, so no classpath-relative banner.txt. Spring's SpringApplicationBannerPrinter resolves banner.txt from the classpath (and spring.banner.location). Rust has no classpath — 'the banner that ships with the app' must be either EMBEDDED at compile time (include_str!/build.rs, the honest analogue — deterministic, no runtime IO, R2/R3) or loaded from a working-dir-relative/operator-supplied FILE PATH at runtime (brittle, the resource-loading neighbour's concern, R1). There is no single mechanism that is both 'ships with the app' and 'overridable by an external file' the way the JVM classpath gives Spring for free — leaf must split these into the embedded case and the explicit-path case.
- No StackWalker main-class deduction for version/title. Spring uses StackWalker(RETAIN_CLASS_REFERENCE) (spring-boot-4-di-design.md line 319) to find the main class for banner version/title metadata. Rust has no runtime stack-class reflection — but it does NOT need it: ${application.version}/${application.title} map cleanly to Cargo metadata (CARGO_PKG_VERSION / CARGO_PKG_NAME, available via env! at compile time or a build-time-populated ApplicationInfo property source), and ${leaf.version} to leaf's own crate metadata. The deduction Spring does at runtime is a COMPILE-TIME-known constant in Rust — a clean win, not a gap.
- No GC; the rendered banner is owned, not a GC'd shared singleton. Spring registers the printed banner as the springBootBanner singleton (a GC'd shared object). In leaf the RenderedBanner is an owned String that must be moved into the registry if registered — and the §2.2 question is whether the singleton ceremony is even rust-native intent (who injects a banner?) or JVM scaffolding to design OUT. No async Drop concern (a banner has no teardown), unlike most container singletons.
- Console-vs-log is a sink/channel choice, not a JVM logger handoff. Spring's banner-mode=log routes the banner through its logging facade (commons-logging/Logback). leaf has no such ambient logger — the Log mode must route through leaf's chosen diagnostics channel (startup-instrumentation's leaf-owned tracing target, events-lifecycle), and Console must write process stdout via an async-correct writer (§2.3), never a naked blocking println that could stall the bootstrap task on a full pipe. The console/log/off tri-state survives as intent; the underlying channels are rust-native.
- ANSI styling + tty detection has no JVM-AnsiOutput analogue and drags in terminal concerns. Spring's AnsiOutput auto/always/never modes detect ANSI capability. leaf must decide tty detection (is stdout a terminal), NO_COLOR honouring, and Windows console handling at the write site — or sidestep color entirely (R2's slim option). This is peripheral but real surface the JVM library handled that leaf must own or drop.
- bindToApplication rebinds banner-mode onto the live driver. Spring binds spring.main.banner-mode back onto the SpringApplication instance mid-run (line 260) so externalized config overrides setBannerMode. leaf inherits this from spring-application-run's bind_to_application seam: the BannerMode must be resolvable by a precedence (default < programmatic setter < bound config), which fights Rust's single-source-of-truth — the print step must read the FINAL bound value, not the programmatic one, coupling the banner to environment's read seam and the run driver's bind step.
- Placeholder resolution is delegated, not fused. Spring's banner printer fuses placeholder substitution into itself. leaf factors it out: the banner template's ${...} placeholders are resolved by handing them to property-resolution's resolve_placeholders walk against the prepared Environment (the SAME engine value-injection uses) — so the banner OWNS the template + ANSI + the output channel, but DELEGATES the ${...} walk, exactly as value-injection delegates its placeholder segment. Who owns the ${...} grammar (placeholder vs literal) is property-resolution's, shared.
- Non-fatal-cosmetic failure is the deliberate exception to fail-fast. Charter §1.7 mandates fail-with-rich-context as early as possible, and the run-pipeline aborts on any throw. But a COSMETIC banner must NOT abort a boot (a missing banner.txt or unresolved placeholder degrading to no-banner is correct UX). So the banner is the one run step where the charter §1.7 fail-fast posture is deliberately RELAXED to degrade-and-warn — an explicit policy that must be stated, not an accident.

### Interactions
- **spring-application-run** — PARENT / the pipeline this step lives inside. The banner IS the print_banner() step of the run pipeline (spring-application-run step 7), fired at a FIXED point: AFTER prepare_environment + EnvironmentPrepared + bind_to_application (so the Environment is prepared and leaf.main.banner-mode is bound), and BEFORE create_context (so it runs before the container exists — the banner cannot be a bean, only registered as one later). The run pipeline owns WHEN print_banner fires and the bind_to_application that resolves the mode; this feature owns WHAT the step does. The RenderedBanner is stashed by the run step for prepare_context to register as a singleton.
- **environment** — DRIVEN. The banner reads the prepared Environment's read view (PropertyResolver) for two things: the leaf.main.banner-mode value (resolving the mode tri-state by precedence) and the placeholder resolution source. Because print_banner fires after prepare_environment, the env is already prepared/sealed — the banner consumes the read view, never mutates. The ApplicationInfo-analogue property source (carrying application.version/title) the banner's placeholders read is the same source Spring's ApplicationInfoPropertySource provides, populated from Cargo metadata.
- **property-resolution** — HARD DELEGATE for placeholder substitution. The banner template's ${application.version}/${leaf.version}/${application.title} (and any user placeholders) are resolved by handing the template (R1) or the placeholder segments (R2) to property-resolution's resolve_placeholders walk — the SAME engine value-injection uses for its ${...} segment. This feature owns the banner template + the placeholder VOCABULARY it documents; property-resolution owns the ${...} walk, defaults, recursion, and strict/lenient policy. A strict unresolved placeholder is a BannerError degraded to a warning by the non-fatal policy.
- **startup-instrumentation** — OUTPUT CHANNEL (Log mode) + INSTRUMENTED. In Log mode the banner routes its rendered lines into startup-instrumentation's leaf-owned tracing target (the events-lifecycle startup-instrumentation R1 tracing-native posture) rather than stdout — unifying 'banner in logs' with leaf's diagnostics substrate. Independently, the print_banner step is itself a child startup-step (leaf.banner.print) nested under the run root (leaf.application.run), with parent linkage carried explicitly (§2.3), so a slow banner shows up in the boot trace.
- **container-core** — REGISTRATION TARGET (if the singleton ceremony is kept). The RenderedBanner is registered as the leafBanner singleton (Spring's springBootBanner) during the run pipeline's prepare_context step, into container-core's context — so it is injectable/queryable after refresh. R1 keeps this for parity; R2/R3 question whether the ceremony is rust-native intent (§2.2) and may drop it. The banner itself fires BEFORE the context exists, so it can never be a bean — only registered into the context afterward.
- **resource-loading** — DELEGATE for the external/text-resource banner (R1's text-resource BannerSource, R3's external-file fallback). Spring's banner.txt / spring.banner.location resolution becomes either compile-time embedding (include_str!, no resource-loading needed — R2/R3 default) or a runtime resource load via the resource-loading neighbour for an operator-supplied path. This couples R1's text-resource source and R3's external-file lane to whatever resource-abstraction leaf settles; R2 sidesteps it entirely via include_str!. (resource-loading is a charter §1 cross-cutting abstraction; its Phase-1 design is the owner of the load primitive.)
- **task-execution** — EXECUTION-CONTEXT SOURCE for the write. The Console-mode write to process stdout uses task-execution's executor (an async writer, or spawn_blocking around a synchronous stdout write if it could block on a full pipe — the §2.3 discipline the banner documents). A custom Banner (R1) or banner_fn closure (R2) that awaits real work runs on the bootstrap task via task-execution. The banner never spawns its own runtime; it borrows the bootstrap task's context.
- **config-data** — INDIRECT (via the EnvironmentPrepared ordering). The leaf.main.banner-mode and any user banner placeholders may come from config-data-loaded sources (application.yaml-analogue). config-data runs at the EnvironmentPrepared hook BEFORE print_banner fires, so by the time the banner reads the mode/placeholders, config-data's loading + precedence assembly is complete. The banner is a consumer of the fully-prepared Environment config-data populated; it has no direct relationship beyond ordering.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is leaf's banner a runtime Banner SPI + source-discovery chain (R1, full Spring parity + replaceable/discoverable, but drags the fragile cross-crate discovery substrate + a resource-loading decision into a cosmetic feature), a compile-time-lowered const + thin mode-match (R2, thinnest/lowest-overhead/cross-crate-inert/full-wasm + honest include_str! analogue, but no discovery and no runtime-authored banner), or a build-time-frozen PartialBanner artifact (R3, near-complete build-time pre-render since banner placeholders are mostly Cargo metadata + earliest fail-fast + DCE-immune, but machinery grossly disproportionate to the feature and pre-judges the compile-vs-runtime keystone hardest)? This is downstream of the §3 compile-vs-runtime + metadata/codegen keystones, and the banner is a deliberately LOW-STAKES place to read the signal — must stay a candidate.
- Does the banner participate in cross-crate auto-DISCOVERY at all, or is it always app-explicit? Every other contribution is auto-discovered, but a banner is an app-level cosmetic essentially never shipped by an integration crate — so R2/R3's instinct to make it explicit-only (no descriptor chain) is defensible, while R1's discovery chain is consistent-but-heavy. Whether the banner is the ONE feature exempted from the discovery substrate, or held uniform for consistency, is a §2.11-vs-§2.2 tension. Cross-cuts the cross-crate composition substrate decision.
- Is the rendered-banner-as-singleton (springBootBanner analogue) rust-native intent or JVM ceremony to design OUT (charter §2.2)? Spring registers it so it is injectable, but no obvious leaf consumer injects a banner (unless an actuator-analogue /info endpoint echoes it). Whether to keep the registration (R1) or drop it (R2/R3 instinct) belongs to the per-feature Phase-3 re-unify, flagged so it is not ported blindly. Cross-cuts container-core's singleton registration.
- How is 'the banner that ships with the app' resolved without a classpath — compile-time embedding (include_str!/build.rs, deterministic, no runtime IO) vs a runtime working-dir/operator-supplied file path (brittle, needs resource-loading) — and is the external-file override (spring.banner.location analogue) even a wanted use case? The embedded case covers the dominant need; the external-file case is real-but-rare and forces a runtime-load lane. This couples to the resource-loading neighbour's abstraction and is the banner's sharpest no-classpath clash. Cross-cuts resource-loading.
- What is the banner's failure posture relative to charter §1.7's fail-fast mandate? A cosmetic banner must NOT abort a boot (degrade-to-no-banner + warn), making it the deliberate EXCEPTION to the run pipeline's abort-on-throw and the §1.7 fail-as-early-as-possible posture. Is 'cosmetic/optional run steps degrade rather than fail' a recognised category in the unified error/diagnostics model, or a banner-specific carve-out? Cross-cuts the unified error/diagnostics keystone and spring-application-run's handle_run_failure.
- How is ANSI styling + terminal capability handled — full Ansi-mode parity (auto/always/never, tty detection, NO_COLOR, Windows console) owned by leaf, delegated to a crate, or dropped (plain text only)? This is peripheral surface the JVM's AnsiOutput handled for Spring; leaf must decide how much terminal-awareness a startup banner warrants. Likely a small, self-contained call but unowned across the Phase-1 catalog.
- Does the Log-mode output channel reuse startup-instrumentation's tracing target, a general logging facade leaf has not yet designed, or a banner-specific channel? The banner-in-logs case wants the same diagnostics substrate as the rest of leaf, but leaf's overall logging/diagnostics output strategy (tracing-native? a log facade?) is itself unsettled across the events-lifecycle area. Belongs to the diagnostics/observability meta-design, not this feature.

### Notes
Scope discipline: I treated this feature strictly as the print_banner() RUN STEP and its three knobs (mode tri-state, resolvable+placeholder-rendered source, output channel) + the deferred singleton registration — building concretely on spring-application-run (which owns WHEN it fires, between EnvironmentPrepared+bind_to_application and create_context, and the bind_to_application that resolves the mode), and delegating to environment/property-resolution (the placeholder ${...} walk + the ApplicationInfo source carrying application.version/title), startup-instrumentation (the Log channel + the leaf.banner.print child step), container-core (the singleton registry, if the ceremony survives), resource-loading (the external/text banner, R1/R3), and task-execution (the async write). It owns ONLY the banner template + placeholder vocabulary, the mode resolution + precedence read, the source selection + ANSI rendering, the channel choice, and the (questioned) singleton stash. This is deliberately a SMALL feature; all three realizations stay implementation-agnostic and hold the deferred §3 keystones (metadata/codegen boundary, compile-vs-runtime split, cross-crate composition substrate, unified error/diagnostics model, the read/mutate env seam, resource-loading abstraction) as CANDIDATES — R1 leans Spring-faithful-runtime-SPI, R2 leans slim-compile-time-lowered-cross-crate-inert, R3 leans build-time-frozen-artifact, deliberately spanning the compile-vs-runtime axis so Phase 2's signal stays unbiased. The banner is UNUSUALLY well-suited to the build-time end (R2/R3): its placeholders are mostly Cargo/leaf METADATA known at compile time (CARGO_PKG_VERSION/NAME via env!), so the run-pipeline R3 instinct pays off more completely here than for a general RunPlan — yet the machinery is grossly disproportionate to a cosmetic feature, which is itself the signal that the banner may be the place where the slim R2 (or even designing the singleton/SPI ceremony OUT per §2.2) is the right rust-native answer. Provider/registry abstractions kept ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC throughout (a native, integration-crate, or test-double Banner/BannerSource participates identically in R1; R2/R3 make it app-explicit). Two §2.2 judgements flagged for Phase 3 and NOT resolved here: (a) whether the Banner SPI + BannerSource discovery chain is rust-native intent or JVM scaffolding (the run-pipeline openNotes explicitly name the banner as 'a print-a-figlet step' to question), and (b) whether the rendered-banner-as-singleton ceremony is wanted at all. The single deliberate charter departure to surface: the banner is the ONE run step where §1.7 fail-fast is RELAXED to degrade-and-warn, because a cosmetic banner must never abort a boot — stated as an explicit policy, and flagged as a phase2 question about whether 'optional/cosmetic steps degrade rather than fail' is a recognised category in the unified error model. Grounding fully cross-checked: spring-boot-4-di-design.md §301-303 (printBanner after env prep before context creation, spring.main.banner-mode console/log/off, SpringApplicationBannerPrinter, banner.txt/spring.banner.location, ${application.version}/${spring-boot.version}/${application.title} placeholders, Ansi, springBootBanner singleton), §244 (the printBanner step's position in run()), §258-260 (prepareEnvironment + ApplicationInfoPropertySource + bindToSpringApplication binding banner-mode), §319 (StackWalker deduction the banner does NOT need in Rust — Cargo metadata is compile-time-known); the charter (DX#1/overhead#2, §1.7 fail-with-context + its deliberate relaxation here, §2.2 intent-not-mechanism + the design-it-OUT license, §2.3 async execution-context, §2.10 thin macros, §2.11 unification, §6 leaf-boot topology); the cross-crate doc (the three DCE layers + the banner as the lowest-stakes discovery surface, Architectures A-D, include_str! as the honest no-classpath analogue, the cross-crate-inert path shared with value-injection); the nightly envelope (nothing needs nightly — const strings, macro placeholder split, env! for build-time metadata, async writer or spawn_blocking for the write, tracing for Log mode, all stable); and the upstream Phase-1 designs (spring-application-run's print_banner step + bind_to_application + RunFailure; environment's read/mutate seam + ApplicationInfo source; value-injection R1's compile-time placeholder-split precedent directly informing R2; startup-instrumentation R1's tracing-native channel for Log mode + the leaf.banner.print child step).

## Exit Code Generation & Application Shutdown  `[exit-code-shutdown]`

leaf's APPLICATION-LEVEL exit-code + shutdown-coordination layer — the SpringApplication.exit() / ExitCodeGenerator / ExitCodeExceptionMapper / ExitCodeEvent + registerShutdownHook surface — strictly ABOVE and orchestrating the container close lifecycle (context-close) it drives, distinct from it. Per spring-boot-4-di-design.md §244 (run() step 2 "enable the shutdown hook"), §313 (handleRunFailure → handleExitCode → listeners.failed → reportFailure → close + rethrow), §890/§407-413/§2400 (registerShutdownHook + the standalone-app 'destruction fires ONLY if you call registerShutdownHook/close' + the v6.1 startupShutdownLock/isStartupShutdownThreadStuck provenance) and the confirmed SpringApplication.exit contract, this feature owns FOUR things that are NOT context-close's job: (1) ARMING the shutdown hook — the registerShutdownHook analogue: an opt-in process-signal (ctrl-c/SIGTERM) listener task armed EARLY in run() (gated by the spring.main.register-shutdown-hook knob bound via bindToApplication) that, once a RunningContext exists, TRIGGERS context-close exactly once; this feature decides WHEN/WHETHER to arm and on WHAT signals, context-close owns the idempotent teardown the hook fires. (2) EXIT-CODE GENERATION — the ExitCodeGenerator role (a bean/descriptor that yields an i32) + the ExitCodeExceptionMapper role (maps a failure cause-type to an i32) + the aggregation rule (collect all generator codes + the failure-derived code; if any non-zero, the highest-magnitude wins — Spring's getExitCode = max-of-positives-or-min-of-negatives; else 0) + the ExitCodeEvent published with the computed code; this is a CORE concern (like availability) not an actuator add-on. (3) APP-LEVEL TEARDOWN ORDERING / the SpringApplication.exit(context, generators…) ANALOGUE — the single coordination point that, on shutdown, drives the ordered app-teardown pass: trigger context-close (which itself stops runtime-lifecycle then destroys beans in reverse dependency order), THEN compute the exit code from the now-closed context's generators, publish ExitCodeEvent, and return the code to the caller. (4) THE EXPLICIT PROCESS-EXIT BOUNDARY — Spring never calls System.exit() inside run(); SpringApplication.exit() RETURNS a code the caller passes to System.exit(); leaf must keep std::process::exit (or returning from main with a Termination/ExitCode) an EXPLICIT, caller-owned, hand-writable boundary, never hidden in a macro. It must replicate this INTENT WITHOUT JVM lineage: no Runtime.addShutdownHook thread (an async tokio::signal listener task on a cold path, not a parked OS thread); no GC fallback for a forgotten close (no async Drop → teardown is the mandatory explicit awaited context-close pass this feature triggers); no synchronized startupShutdownLock / isStartupShutdownThreadStuck interrupt (the hook-vs-explicit-close race is dissolved by context-close's idempotent CAS/state-machine, and a hung teardown is bounded by per-bean/per-phase timeouts + async cancellation, never an interrupted thread); no reflective instanceof scan for ExitCodeGenerator beans (origin-agnostic descriptors / typed collection from a scoped registry, never a runtime type-walk); and the exit-code-from-exception mapping rides leaf's rust-native error model (typed-cause matching / downcast), not Spring's reflective AbstractFailureAnalyzer-style generic matching. It sits inside the spring-application-run pipeline: run() arms the hook (this feature) at step 2 and on failure routes through handleRunFailure where handleExitCode (this feature) computes the code before close; the steady-state shutdown path is hook-fires → context-close → exit-code compute → ExitCodeEvent → return-code-to-caller.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Three thin roles + an exit() coordinator over context-close
Mirror Spring's surface 1:1 as three small origin-agnostic roles plus one coordinator, each thin over the already-designed context-close/run pipeline. ROLE A — `ExitCodeContributor { fn exit_code(&self) -> i32 }` (the ExitCodeGenerator analogue): a bean OR a thin const descriptor that yields an exit code, collected from the SAME scoped catalog substrate the rest of leaf uses (linkme/codegen/explicit, Phase-2-undecided), addressed by role, never a runtime instanceof scan. ROLE B — `ExceptionExitMapper { fn map(&self, cause: &LeafError) -> Option<i32> }` (the ExitCodeExceptionMapper analogue): maps a failure cause to a code via leaf's rust-native error model (a downcast / typed-enum match over the cause-chain, NOT reflective type-generics) — and an error type may itself implement `ExitCodeContributor` so 'the exception carries its own code' is just role A on the error. ROLE C — the SHUTDOWN HOOK: an opt-in `async fn arm_shutdown_hook(signals: &[Signal], on_signal: impl FnOnce -> close())` that spawns a cold-path task awaiting a tokio::signal future (ctrl-c/SIGTERM) and, on first fire, calls context-close ONCE (context-close's idempotent CAS makes a racing explicit close a defined no-op). Arming is gated by the bound `leaf.main.register-shutdown-hook` knob (default on for a long-running app, off for a one-shot) read from the prepared Environment; the signal-listener is runtime-specific so it lives behind a thin seam likely realized in leaf-tokio, the core defining only the `ShutdownTrigger` contract. THE COORDINATOR — `async fn exit(context: &RunningContext, extra: &[&dyn ExitCodeContributor]) -> i32` (the SpringApplication.exit() analogue): (1) await context-close (which stops runtime-lifecycle then destroys beans reverse-order — this feature does NOT tear down beans, it TRIGGERS the close); (2) collect every ExitCodeContributor (from the context's generators + the `extra` slice + any failure-derived code from the mappers) into one set; (3) AGGREGATE via Spring's rule — if all zero → 0, else the highest-magnitude code (max of positives, or min of negatives) wins; (4) publish `ExitCodeEvent { code }` via events; (5) RETURN the i32 — never call process::exit. On the FAILURE path, the run pipeline's handle_run_failure calls this feature's `handle_exit_code(cause)` which runs the ExceptionExitMapper chain (+ error-as-contributor) to derive a code, fires the failed/ExitCodeEvent, then drives context-close — exactly Spring's handleExitCode→failed→reportFailure→close order. The PROCESS-EXIT boundary stays explicit and hand-writable: `#[leaf::main]` emits a visible `std::process::exit(app.run(args).await.map(|ctx| leaf::exit(&ctx, &[])).unwrap_or_else(|f| f.exit_code()))`-shaped tail (or returns a `std::process::ExitCode` from main via Termination), nothing hidden — the macro only emits what a user could write by hand.

**Pros:** Highest intent fidelity at lowest cost: ExitCodeContributor↔ExitCodeGenerator, ExceptionExitMapper↔ExitCodeExceptionMapper, the arm-hook step↔registerShutdownHook, and exit()↔SpringApplication.exit() map 1:1 — a Spring user finds the exact four concepts, and cargo expand of #[leaf::main] reads like the hand-written run-then-exit tail (DX #1, §2.10, intent-not-mechanism parity).; Clean delegation, not reinvention (§2.11): exit() TRIGGERS context-close (which owns stop-then-destroy reverse-order teardown) and asks events to publish ExitCodeEvent — this feature stays a thin coordinator whose surface is the three roles + the aggregation rule + the hook arming, never container/teardown internals; the cancel-vs-close fork and per-bean timeouts are wholly context-close's.; The explicit RETURN-the-code / caller-owns-process::exit boundary is the honest rust-native port of Spring's deliberate 'run() never calls System.exit, exit() returns a code' design — it composes perfectly with Rust's Termination/ExitCode-from-main story, keeps the macro thin (nothing hidden), and means a library/embedded host can compute the code without the framework ever killing the process (§2.10 escape hatches).; Async-first + correct execution context (§2.3): the hook is a cold-path tokio::signal task (never a parked OS thread, Spring's Runtime.addShutdownHook dissolved), exit() awaits context-close as one unit, and the hook-vs-explicit-close race is a defined no-op via context-close's idempotent CAS — so two close triggers cannot contend a lock (Spring's startupShutdownLock dissolved by execution context, §2.5).; Origin-agnostic by construction: an ExitCodeContributor / ExceptionExitMapper from a native crate, a WASM-module, or a test double participates identically (descriptor by role), and the error-as-contributor case (an error type implementing ExitCodeContributor) collapses 'exception carries its own code' into role A with no special path — minimal, uniform surface.; Exit-code-as-CORE (not actuator), mirroring availability-as-core: the aggregation + ExitCodeEvent live in leaf-boot's run layer, so a plain CLI app gets meaningful exit codes with zero ecosystem deps — exactly the charter §1 'core never depends on web/actuator' posture.

**Cons:** Three separate roles + a coordinator is the most surface for what is conceptually one concern (turn shutdown into a number + fire it) — a smaller design might fuse the mapper into the contributor or fold exit-code into the run pipeline's failure result, so R1 risks more named concepts than the intent strictly needs (a DX-simplicity tension for the 90% CLI app that just wants 'return non-zero on error').; The exit-code AGGREGATION RULE (highest-magnitude wins; max-of-positives-or-min-of-negatives) is a Spring-specific, mildly surprising convention hand-maintained in one function — it is a correctness property with no structural guard, and a contributor returning an unexpected sign/magnitude can silently dominate the code (the same opacity Spring has, now leaf's to document and test).; handle_exit_code on the FAILURE path must coordinate ordering with context-close AND the FailureAnalyzer chain AND the failed-event — it sits in the same handle_run_failure tangle the run pipeline owns (which teardown on which failure, AbandonedRunException short-circuit skips both analysis AND exit-code), inheriting that coordination burden as a cross-feature seam.; The ExceptionExitMapper matching mechanism (downcast over a typed-error enum vs Any vs pattern-matched cause-chain) is downstream of the deferred unified error/diagnostics keystone — R1 must keep it a candidate, so the role's exact signature (`&LeafError` vs `&dyn Any` vs a structured cause) can't be pinned here without biasing Phase 2.; The signal-listener seam is runtime-specific (tokio::signal vs smol vs none) so the hook's concrete realization lives in an integration crate, meaning the core's `ShutdownTrigger` contract must be designed to bridge multiple signal sources — and a core-only / no-runtime binary has NO hook (must still tear down via explicit close), a scope split the design must state clearly.; Exit-code contributors inherit the cross-crate DCE silent-failure hazard QUIETLY: a missing contributor in a DCE'd crate doesn't crash — it just yields a wrong (often zero) exit code, an even quieter failure than a missing bean, mandating the anti-DCE self-check but with a failure mode that won't surface in lib tests (only in the final-binary exit status).

**Mechanisms considered:** `ExitCodeContributor { fn exit_code(&self) -> i32 }` (ExitCodeGenerator analogue) collected as origin-agnostic role descriptors / beans from the scoped catalog substrate, never a runtime instanceof scan; `ExceptionExitMapper { fn map(&self, cause: &LeafError) -> Option<i32> }` (ExitCodeExceptionMapper analogue) matching over leaf's rust-native error model (downcast / typed-enum / cause-chain), plus an error type implementing ExitCodeContributor directly (exception-carries-its-own-code); opt-in `arm_shutdown_hook` — a cold-path tokio::signal listener task (ctrl-c/SIGTERM) that triggers context-close ONCE, gated by the bound leaf.main.register-shutdown-hook knob; runtime-specific seam (ShutdownTrigger contract in core, impl in leaf-tokio); `async fn exit(context, extra: &[&dyn ExitCodeContributor]) -> i32` (SpringApplication.exit analogue): await context-close → collect contributors + failure code → aggregate (highest-magnitude wins) → publish ExitCodeEvent → RETURN code (never process::exit); Spring's aggregation rule as an explicit function: all-zero→0 else max-of-positives-or-min-of-negatives (highest absolute value); `handle_exit_code(cause)` on the failure path: run ExceptionExitMapper chain + error-as-contributor → derive code → fire failed + ExitCodeEvent → drive context-close, in Spring's handleExitCode→failed→reportFailure→close order, with AbandonedRunException short-circuiting both; explicit caller-owned process-exit boundary: #[leaf::main] emits a visible std::process::exit(...) / returns std::process::ExitCode via Termination — thin macro, nothing hidden; ExitCodeEvent { code } published via the events substrate (exit-code-as-core, like availability)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** leaf-core/leaf-boot defines the `ExitCodeContributor`/`ExceptionExitMapper` role traits, the `ExitCodeEvent` type + its stable name, the `ShutdownTrigger` contract, the aggregation rule, and the `exit()`/`handle_exit_code()` coordinator (charter §6: this is leaf-boot, the SpringApplication-equivalent). ExitCodeContributors that are BEANS are a runtime byproduct of the container (no extra discovery surface — collected from the live context like Spring collects ExitCodeGenerator beans); ExitCodeContributors/ExceptionExitMappers contributed as link-time DESCRIPTORS (e.g. a framework-default mapper an integration crate ships) ride the SAME catalog substrate the rest of leaf uses (Phase-2-undecided linkme distributed-slice / build.rs codegen / explicit with(); cross-crate doc Architectures A-D), addressed by role, never a runtime type-scan (Spring's instanceof ExitCodeGenerator is impossible). This INHERITS the DCE hazard but in its QUIETEST form (cross-crate doc Layer 0 unlinked-crate, Layer A rustc-reachability pre-1.62, Layer B --gc-sections under LLD pre-1.89): a missing contributor/mapper yields a WRONG exit code (often 0), not a crash — so the feature must participate in the run pipeline's mandated anti-DCE self-check (expected-vs-found mappers/contributors per source crate) and is a natural consumer of 'leaf doctor'/startup-report. Generics can't carry a 'static descriptor → a register_exit_mapper!/register_exit_code!(Concrete) shim hard-errors with an escape hatch. The signal-listener (tokio::signal) is RUNTIME-SPECIFIC and cannot live in the runtime-agnostic core (§6): the core defines `ShutdownTrigger`, leaf-tokio (or the chosen runtime crate) supplies the concrete signal-future listener — so a core-only binary has no auto-hook and must call exit()/close() explicitly. The `ExitCodeEvent` name + the role-trait signatures become a small stable cross-crate ABI (like the run-listener event-name taxonomy / startup-instrumentation's name vocabulary), emitted with absolute paths by the thin macros. The process-exit boundary is binary-crate-local (the generated #[leaf::main] tail), not cross-crate.

**T2 — execution context:** Entirely COLD-PATH (startup-arm + shutdown), but with explicit async discipline (§2.3). The shutdown hook is a SEPARATE cold task awaiting a tokio::signal future (ctrl-c/SIGTERM) — never a parked OS thread (Spring's Runtime.addShutdownHook thread dissolved); on first fire it triggers context-close ONCE (close-once is context-close's CAS / state transition, not a lock — §2.5). exit() runs on whatever task invokes it (the bootstrap task on the failure path, or the hook task / an explicit caller on the steady-state path): it AWAITS context-close as one all-or-nothing unit (which awaits runtime-lifecycle stop_all() then the reverse-order destroy walk under per-bean timeouts — this feature does not own those timeouts), THEN synchronously aggregates the collected i32 codes (pure cold-path computation, no IO), publishes ExitCodeEvent (ordered dispatch, may await listeners), and RETURNS the code. A contributor's exit_code() / mapper's map() is expected to be cheap+sync (a number-yielding query, not IO); if a contributor needs async work it must have done it before shutdown. NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5): the only serialization is context-close's own single-container close-once gate; the hook-vs-explicit-close race resolves to a defined no-op via that gate, never a contended mutex (Spring's startupShutdownLock + isStartupShutdownThreadStuck interrupt dissolved — a hung teardown is bounded by context-close's per-bean/per-phase timeouts + async cancellation, never an interrupted thread). CANCELLATION: cancelling exit() mid-close routes into context-close's cancellation (partial teardown recorded in its ShutdownReport); the exit code in that case is best-effort (a defined 'aborted teardown' code or the failure-derived code). NO async Drop → teardown is the explicit awaited context-close pass this feature triggers, never a Drop (nightly doc). The PROCESS-EXIT call (std::process::exit / returning ExitCode) is the FINAL execution-context boundary, owned by the caller/generated main tail AFTER exit() returns — the framework never kills the process itself, so embedded/test hosts observe the code without termination.

#### R2 — Shutdown as a single observable Termination state-machine (ShutdownController over a reactive watch)
Question whether exit-code generation and hook-arming are best modeled as separate procedures (R1). Instead unify them into ONE explicit, total, observable application-shutdown state machine — a `ShutdownController` holding `TerminationState = Running | Triggered(reason) | Closing | ExitCodeComputed(i32) | Terminated(i32)` behind a `tokio::sync::watch<TerminationState>` (REACTIVE, §2.4: transitions notify subscribers via wakers, never a polled flag). SHUTDOWN TRIGGERS are typed INPUTS to the machine: `ShutdownReason = Signal(SIGTERM|CtrlC) | ExplicitClose | RunCompleted | RunFailed(LeafError) | Abandoned`. The shutdown hook (arm step) is just a cold task that, on a signal future, sends a `Triggered(Signal)` event into the controller — and because the controller serializes events, a signal arriving WHILE an explicit close is in flight is an enumerated 'already-Triggered → coalesce' transition (Spring's hook-vs-explicit-close race designed OUT, not raced). The transition Running→Triggered→Closing AWAITS context-close (stop runtime-lifecycle, reverse-order destroy — context-close still owns the teardown); Closing→ExitCodeComputed collects every ExitCodeContributor + the failure-derived code (via the ExceptionExitMapper chain, which is an input to the machine) and aggregates (highest-magnitude wins), publishing ExitCodeEvent as the transition side-effect; ExitCodeComputed→Terminated(code) is the terminal state holding the final i32. The machine is TOTAL: a second trigger while Closing AWAITS the in-flight termination via the watch channel (idempotency as a defined transition, no lock); Abandoned short-circuits straight to Terminated WITHOUT close or exit-code (the AbandonedRunException analogue as an enumerated transition); RunFailed carries its cause into the ExceptionExitMapper input so the failure code is computed in the same machine. PROCESS-EXIT stays explicit: the caller awaits `controller.terminated()` (a watch await) to get the final i32 and passes it to std::process::exit — the controller NEVER calls process::exit; it only computes and publishes the code. Readiness/operations tooling SUBSCRIBES to TerminationState ('am I shutting down?') instead of polling — the same reactive-state shape runtime-lifecycle R3 and context-close R3 use, so the whole bring-up/teardown shares one observable-state-machine substrate.

**Pros:** Makes the hook-vs-explicit-close race, idempotency, the abandoned short-circuit, and signal-during-close all FIRST-CLASS enumerated transitions with defined semantics — directly designing OUT Spring's startupShutdownLock / isStartupShutdownThreadStuck tangle and the double-trigger race rather than documenting around them (the deepest §2.9 win for the shutdown-coordination intent).; Reactive shutdown observation (§2.4): operations/readiness tooling SUBSCRIBES to TerminationState ('am I draining? closing? terminated with what code?') via a watch channel woken on transition, never polling a flag — and ExitCodeEvent becomes a transition side-effect, unifying shutdown observation with the event bus (§2.11).; Strong unification with the upstream lifecycle designs: this is the SAME observable-state-machine + watch-channel substrate runtime-lifecycle R3 (RunState) and context-close R3 (RunDown) propose — so bring-up, teardown, and termination/exit-code share ONE reactive-state primitive across the whole application lifecycle, the explicit reason Phases 2-4 exist (§2.11).; Single coordination point dissolves Spring's lock WITHOUT a mutex (§2.5): all shutdown triggers (signal, explicit, run-failed, abandoned) are serialized by the single controller, so concurrent triggers AWAIT the in-flight termination via the channel — the cleanest no-global-lock posture for the multi-trigger shutdown case, and a hung close is bounded by context-close's timeouts surfacing as a legible stuck-in-Closing state.; The explicit TerminationState is a superb diagnostic + test surface: at any instant 'what is the app's shutdown doing, triggered by what, with what code' is one typed value + a transition history — far richer than Spring's scattered booleans, and the final exit code's provenance (which reason, which contributor dominated) is inspectable, not emergent.; Still keeps the process-exit boundary explicit and caller-owned (await terminated() → process::exit): the controller computes+publishes the code but never kills the process, so embedded/test/library hosts observe the full termination without termination — same §2.10 escape-hatch win as R1, now with a richer observable progression.

**Cons:** Heaviest conceptual surface and the largest divergence from Spring's model (Spring has NO application-termination state machine — just SpringApplication.exit() + a shutdown-hook thread + getExitCode arithmetic): modeling a 5-state total machine is genuinely MORE than the intent needs for a CLI that just returns non-zero, risking over-engineering the 90% case (DX-simplicity tension, §2.1).; Building on a watch-channel + single-controller substrate PRE-JUDGES the deferred async-execution / structured-concurrency / actor keystone (§3): whether leaf even has a controller-task or reactive-state primitive is a Phase-2 call, and three lifecycle features (runtime-lifecycle, context-close, this) each proposing a controller-state-machine R-variant biases that signal — must stay a CANDIDATE, and the three machines must be RECONCILED in Phase 3 (are RunState, RunDown, TerminationState one machine or three layered ones?).; Totality is a specification BURDEN: enumerating EVERY (state, trigger) pair (signal-during-ExitCodeComputed, explicit-close-after-Terminated, run-failed-while-already-Triggered-by-signal) is a large correctness surface that LOOKS total but can have gaps — an incompletely-specified machine implies guarantees it may not keep, worse than R1's simple procedure.; The watch<TerminationState> latest-value-wins semantics can DROP intermediate transitions a subscriber cared about (a known watch pitfall) — fine for 'are we terminated + what code' but lossy for 'observe every shutdown step', possibly needing a broadcast/event-log alongside, more machinery for the observation story.; Coupling ExitCodeEvent to a transition side-effect ties this feature tightly to context-close R3 and lifecycle-events' realization; if the event model lands as typed channels rather than a bus, the watch channel + event bus become two overlapping notification mechanisms (redundancy, §2.11 working against itself) — and the unification only pays off if Phase 2 actually picks the reactive-state substrate.; Folding exit-code-generation INTO the termination machine means the exit-code roles (ExitCodeContributor / ExceptionExitMapper) become inputs to a controller rather than free-standing collected roles — slightly obscuring the simple 'these beans yield codes' Spring mental model behind a state-machine framing a Spring user won't expect.

**Mechanisms considered:** `TerminationState = Running | Triggered(reason) | Closing | ExitCodeComputed(i32) | Terminated(i32)` as a TOTAL state machine behind a tokio::sync::watch (reactive, §2.4 — transitions wake subscribers, never a polled flag); typed `ShutdownReason = Signal | ExplicitClose | RunCompleted | RunFailed(LeafError) | Abandoned` as the machine's INPUTS; the shutdown hook is a cold signal task that sends Triggered(Signal) into the controller; a single `ShutdownController` serializing all triggers so a concurrent/racing trigger AWAITS the in-flight termination (idempotency + hook-vs-explicit-close race + abandoned short-circuit as ENUMERATED transitions; no lock, §2.5); Closing transition AWAITS context-close (stop-then-destroy still owned by context-close); ExitCodeComputed collects ExitCodeContributors + failure code (ExceptionExitMapper chain as an input) and aggregates highest-magnitude-wins, publishing ExitCodeEvent as the transition side-effect; caller awaits `controller.terminated() -> i32` via the watch channel then calls std::process::exit — controller never kills the process; operations/readiness tooling SUBSCRIBES to TerminationState ('am I shutting down?') instead of polling; SAME observable-state-machine + watch substrate as runtime-lifecycle R3 (RunState) and context-close R3 (RunDown) — one reactive lifecycle primitive across bring-up/teardown/termination (§2.11), reconciled in Phase 3; Abandoned → Terminated WITHOUT close/exit-code (AbandonedRunException analogue as an enumerated transition)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same role-collection T1 as R1 (leaf-core/leaf-boot defines the roles, ExitCodeEvent, ShutdownReason/TerminationState; contributors are runtime beans or link-time descriptors via the Phase-2 catalog substrate; the same QUIET DCE hazard for a missing contributor/mapper → wrong exit code, mandating the anti-DCE self-check; generics hard-error with a register shim; tokio::signal hook is runtime-specific → core defines the trigger contract, leaf-tokio supplies the signal source). The DISTINCTIVE cross-crate surface is the `watch_termination() -> Receiver<TerminationState>` observation channel: a thin, stable, origin-agnostic API that operations tooling, integration crates ('flush my buffers when we enter Closing'), and external probes consume — a WASM-module participant or an external probe observes termination identically to a native one. Because the controller serializes transitions, NO contributing crate may drive termination out-of-band — only the controller does; contributors/mappers are PASSIVE inputs (collected and called), keeping the cross-crate contract clean (passive roles + an observation channel, never a back-channel). The notable cross-crate RISK is that the controller/watch substrate ASSUMES Phase 2 provides a reactive-state / controller primitive — if leaf's async model is task-based-without-controllers, this becomes an explicit code-driven progression + a notify, still cross-crate-clean but a different internal substrate (a phase2Question), and it SHARES that substrate with runtime-lifecycle R3 + context-close R3 across crate boundaries (a unification opportunity that must be coordinated in Phase 3). The ExitCodeEvent name + TerminationState enum + the role traits become a slightly WIDER stable ABI than R1 (the state vocabulary is now public), versioned cross-crate. The process-exit boundary remains binary-crate-local.

**T2 — execution context:** The CONTROLLER is a single owned task (or the shutdown-caller's task acting as the serialization point) that OWNS TerminationState and processes shutdown TRIGGERS one-at-a-time, so concurrent callers (a signal firing while an explicit close is in flight) AWAIT the in-flight termination via the watch/mailbox rather than racing (no lock, §2.5 — serialization by single-ownership). The shutdown hook is a SEPARATE cold task awaiting tokio::signal that merely SENDS a Triggered(Signal) event (never a parked OS thread). The Running→Triggered→Closing transition AWAITS context-close as one unit (which awaits runtime-lifecycle stop_all() then the reverse-order destroy under per-bean timeouts — owned by context-close, not here); Closing→ExitCodeComputed runs the SYNCHRONOUS cold-path aggregation over the collected i32 codes and publishes ExitCodeEvent (waking watch subscribers, §2.4 reactive); →Terminated(code) is terminal. NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5): the only serialization is single-controller-ownership; the watch channel is the §2.4 notification primitive (termination progress is never busy-waited). CANCELLATION: cancelling a transition (a hung close) is handled inside the controller via context-close's own cancellation + timeouts, transitioning to a defined state (stuck-in-Closing surfaced as a rich diagnostic naming the stalled bean), never an interrupted OS thread (Spring's isStartupShutdownThreadStuck dissolved). NO async Drop → teardown is context-close's explicit awaited pass the Closing transition drives. The caller awaits `terminated()` (a watch await, cold path) to obtain the final i32 and performs the FINAL process-exit boundary (std::process::exit / return ExitCode) AFTER the controller reaches Terminated — the controller never kills the process, so the termination is fully observable by embedded/test hosts. ERRORS: a RunFailed trigger carries its cause into the ExceptionExitMapper input so the failure-derived code is computed in the same machine; an Abandoned trigger short-circuits to Terminated without close or exit-code.

#### R3 — Termination as a leaf-native typed-result + std::process::Termination (minimize bespoke machinery)
Question whether leaf needs Spring's ExitCodeGenerator/Mapper/exit() surface AT ALL as distinct concepts, or whether it is JVM-shaped scaffolding rust-native idioms make largely unnecessary (intent-parity re-judgement, charter §2.2). Push the rust-native answer hardest: model the WHOLE thing as the run pipeline's typed RESULT + the standard library's `std::process::Termination`/`ExitCode` traits, folding exit-code derivation into leaf's unified error model rather than a parallel SPI. The run pipeline returns `Result<RunningContext, RunFailure>`; this feature defines `enum AppOutcome { Ran(RunningContext), Failed(RunFailure) }` and a thin `async fn run_to_outcome(...) -> AppOutcome`. EXIT-CODE DERIVATION is a method on the leaf-native types: `RunFailure::exit_code(&self) -> ExitCode` walks the failure's typed cause-chain (the unified error model already carries structured cause + context per §1.7/§2.9) and returns the first mapped code — so the ExceptionExitMapper role collapses into 'the error model knows its own exit code' (an error variant can carry an explicit code; a default maps known boot-failure categories — port-in-use, missing-bean, bad-binding — to conventional codes, reusing the FailureAnalysis taxonomy). For a SUCCESSFUL run, exit-code contributors are simply beans/values the RunningContext exposes: `RunningContext::exit_code()` collects any `ExitCodeContributor` and aggregates (highest-magnitude wins). The SHUTDOWN HOOK remains an opt-in `arm_shutdown_hook` (the one genuinely-needed orchestration: a signal listener triggering context-close), but it is the ONLY bespoke shutdown machinery — and even it can be hand-written by a user wiring tokio::signal + ctx.close() directly (the macro just emits that). The PROCESS-EXIT boundary becomes the std-idiomatic one: `main` returns `impl Termination` (or a leaf `ExitCode`), so `fn main() -> leaf::ExitCode { leaf::block_on(app.run(args).await.into_termination()) }` — the rust-native 'return a Termination from main' replaces SpringApplication.exit(); the runtime calls the std exit path. ExitCodeEvent becomes a lifecycle-event the RunningContext fires before close (for listeners that care), but it is NOT load-bearing for computing the code (the code is the typed-result's Termination). This deliberately DELETES the standalone exit() coordinator + the separate mapper SPI, betting that leaf's unified error model + std's Termination already carry the intent and that the only irreducible new concept is the signal-hook trigger.

**Pros:** Maximal rust-native intent-parity (§2.2): exit-code-from-failure becomes 'the error knows its code' on leaf's unified error model, and process-exit becomes std's Termination/ExitCode-from-main — so a Spring concept that exists partly to route around the JVM (a separate ExitCodeGenerator SPI + a static exit() helper + System.exit) is realized with idioms Rust already has, deleting bespoke machinery (the charter's 'may realize it completely differently — or prove it unnecessary').; Lowest overhead + smallest surface (§2.1 #2, §2.11): no parallel exit-code SPI, no exit() coordinator, no termination state machine — exit-code is a method on types that already exist (RunFailure / RunningContext) and the only new concept is the opt-in signal hook; the 90% CLI app gets correct non-zero-on-error exit codes essentially for free via into_termination().; Tightest unification with the deferred unified error/diagnostics keystone (§1.7/§2.9): the exit code rides the SAME typed cause-chain + FailureAnalysis taxonomy the failure-analysis surface already builds, so 'port-in-use → analysis + action + a conventional exit code' is ONE coherent story rather than two parallel SPIs (FailureAnalyzer for the message, ExitCodeExceptionMapper for the number) — the highest-leverage consolidation of the charter's complete-context-failure promise.; Most idiomatic process-exit boundary: `fn main() -> leaf::ExitCode` via std::process::Termination is the canonical Rust pattern, composes with `?` in main and with the test harness (tests observe the ExitCode without process death), and keeps #[leaf::main] the THINnest of the three (it emits a main returning a Termination — nothing a user couldn't hand-write, §2.10).; Async-first, no-async-Drop-native, no-global-lock identical to R1 for the parts it keeps: the signal hook is a cold tokio::signal task triggering context-close's idempotent close; teardown is context-close's explicit awaited pass; the hook-vs-explicit-close race is a defined no-op via context-close's CAS (§2.3/§2.5).; Origin-agnostic and DCE-resilient where it matters: because exit-code-on-failure is carried BY the error value (not collected from a registry), a failure's code is NEVER silently lost to DCE (R1/R2's quiet-wrong-code hazard) — only the optional success-path ExitCodeContributor collection inherits the (milder) DCE concern, shrinking the silent-failure surface.

**Cons:** Diverges MOST from Spring's surface and DELETES recognizable concepts (no SpringApplication.exit(), no ExitCodeGenerator/ExitCodeExceptionMapper SPI as such) — a Spring user migrating finds the intent realized but not the named hooks, and a library that legitimately wants 'register a bean that contributes an exit code at shutdown' (a real Spring use case, e.g. a batch job's status) is awkward if exit-code is mostly a property of the typed result rather than a collectible bean role (a fidelity + extensibility cost).; PRE-JUDGES the deferred unified error/diagnostics keystone (§3) the HARDEST of the three: folding exit-code-derivation into the error model assumes that model carries a typed cause-chain with mappable categories AND an exit-code slot — but that model is explicitly Phase-2-undecided, so R3's core bet (exit-code is a method on RunFailure) can't be validated here and risks biasing Phase 2 toward a specific error representation (must stay strictly a CANDIDATE).; The success-path exit-code (a long-running server that wants to exit non-zero on a controlled shutdown, or a batch job signalling status) is the AWKWARD case: 'the code is the typed result's Termination' fits a CLI that runs-then-exits, but a server whose run() returns a RunningContext and exits LATER (via the hook) has no natural typed-result to carry the code — so R3 still needs the RunningContext::exit_code() contributor collection, partially reintroducing the SPI it sought to delete (the bet only fully pays off for run-to-completion apps).; ExitCodeEvent demoted to a non-load-bearing lifecycle event means a listener that wants to OBSERVE the final code (Spring's ExitCodeEvent use case — log it, report it) must hook a different surface than the one that COMPUTES it, a split R1/R2 avoid by making the event the computed code's publication — a subtle two-source-of-truth for 'what was the exit code'.; Relying on std::process::Termination/ExitCode constrains the code SPACE (std ExitCode is portable 0-255-ish, not an arbitrary i32) and the Termination trait's behavior is the std runtime's, not leaf's — so Spring's arbitrary-i32 + highest-magnitude-aggregation semantics don't map cleanly onto std ExitCode, forcing leaf to either define its own ExitCode (back to bespoke) or accept a narrower code model than Spring's.; Minimizing machinery means the hook-vs-explicit-close + multi-trigger coordination that R2 makes first-class is left as 'context-close's CAS handles it' — adequate, but R3 gives up R2's observable termination progression and enumerated-race guarantees, so a complex shutdown (signal during a controlled drain during a failure) has less structural support than R2 (a robustness-vs-minimalism tradeoff).

**Mechanisms considered:** model termination as the run pipeline's typed RESULT: `enum AppOutcome { Ran(RunningContext), Failed(RunFailure) }` + `run_to_outcome()`; exit-code is a METHOD on these types, not a separate SPI; `RunFailure::exit_code()` walks the unified error model's typed cause-chain (an error variant carries an explicit code; defaults map FailureAnalysis categories — port-in-use/missing-bean/bad-binding — to conventional codes) — the ExceptionExitMapper role collapses into 'the error knows its code'; `RunningContext::exit_code()` collects optional ExitCodeContributor beans/values and aggregates (highest-magnitude wins) for the success/controlled-shutdown path; process-exit via std::process::Termination/ExitCode: `fn main() -> leaf::ExitCode { ... app.run(...).into_termination() }` — the rust-native replacement for SpringApplication.exit() + System.exit; thinnest macro; opt-in `arm_shutdown_hook` (cold tokio::signal task → context-close) as the ONLY irreducible bespoke shutdown machinery — hand-writable directly; ExitCodeEvent demoted to a non-load-bearing lifecycle event (observe-only), NOT the source of truth for the code; DELETE the standalone exit() coordinator + the separate ExitCodeExceptionMapper SPI, betting unified-error-model + std-Termination carry the intent (intent-parity re-judgement per §2.2); leaf-defined ExitCode newtype IF std ExitCode's 0-255 portable space is too narrow for Spring's arbitrary-i32 + sign-magnitude aggregation

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The SMALLEST cross-crate surface of the three. Exit-code-on-failure is carried BY the RunFailure value (the unified error model's type, defined in leaf-core), so it needs NO cross-crate discovery and is NEVER subject to the DCE silent-failure hazard (a failure's code travels with the error, not a registry) — a key T1 simplification that removes R1/R2's quietest failure mode for the dominant (failure) case. The success-path ExitCodeContributor collection is the ONLY part that rides the Phase-2 catalog substrate (linkme/codegen/explicit; Architectures A-D) and inherits the milder DCE concern (a missing success-path contributor → a wrong success code), still participating in the run pipeline's anti-DCE self-check. Folding exit-code into the error model COUPLES this feature cross-crate to the unified error/diagnostics model's representation (the error type's cause-chain + exit-code slot must be a stable leaf-core surface every crate's errors flow through) — a tight coupling that is a unification dividend IF Phase 2 lands that model, a risk if it lands differently (a phase2Question). The std::process::Termination/ExitCode boundary is binary-crate-local and platform-portable (no linker/DCE concern), and is the cross-crate doc's deterministic-portable posture for the exit step (no link-time registry needed for the failure code at all). The tokio::signal hook is runtime-specific (core defines the trigger contract, leaf-tokio supplies it) exactly as R1/R2. ExitCodeEvent's name remains a small stable ABI for observe-only listeners. Generics-cannot-register applies only to the optional success-path contributor (register shim). Net: R3 moves the load-bearing exit-code path OFF the cross-crate discovery substrate entirely (onto the typed error value + std Termination), shrinking the cross-crate attack surface to the optional success-path contributor + the runtime-specific hook.

**T2 — execution context:** COLD-PATH throughout, with the LEAST bespoke async machinery. The signal hook is a cold tokio::signal task triggering context-close ONCE (idempotent via context-close's CAS; never a parked OS thread; §2.3). On the FAILURE path the exit code is computed SYNCHRONOUSLY off the RunFailure value (walking the typed cause-chain — pure cold-path, no IO) the instant run() returns Err, with no registry collection needed; on the SUCCESS/controlled-shutdown path RunningContext::exit_code() synchronously aggregates collected contributor codes AFTER context-close (which this feature triggers via the hook or an explicit close) has awaited runtime-lifecycle stop_all() + the reverse-order destroy (owned by context-close, per-bean timeouts there). NO GLOBAL LOCK (§2.5): the only serialization is context-close's close-once gate; the hook-vs-explicit-close race resolves to a defined no-op via that gate. NO async Drop → teardown is context-close's explicit awaited pass. The PROCESS-EXIT boundary is std::process::Termination/ExitCode returned from main — the std runtime performs the exit AFTER leaf's async block_on returns the ExitCode; leaf never calls process::exit itself inside the framework (the caller's main signature owns it), so tests/embedded hosts observe the ExitCode without process death (§2.10). CANCELLATION: cancelling mid-shutdown routes into context-close's cancellation + timeouts (partial teardown in its ShutdownReport); the resulting exit code is the failure-derived code or a defined 'aborted' code. ExitCodeEvent (observe-only) is fired on the bootstrap/shutdown task as a lifecycle event before close, ordered, never busy-waited (§2.4) — but it is NOT on the critical path for computing the code. ERRORS propagate as the typed RunFailure carrying both the FailureAnalysis context AND its exit code (§2.9) — one coherent value, the unified-error-model bet.

### Rust clashes
- No JVM Runtime.addShutdownHook thread. Spring's registerShutdownHook spawns a JVM thread that runs doClose() on SIGTERM/process-exit. Rust/leaf has no equivalent thread-parking hook and is async-first: the analogue is an OPT-IN cold-path task awaiting a tokio::signal future (ctrl-c/SIGTERM) that triggers context-close ONCE — a cold await, not a parked OS thread. The signal source is runtime-specific (tokio::signal vs smol vs none), so the listener lives behind a thin ShutdownTrigger seam in an integration crate (leaf-tokio), NOT the runtime-agnostic core (charter §6); a core-only / no-runtime binary has no auto-hook and must tear down via an explicit awaited close.
- run() never calls System.exit, and exit() RETURNS a code — the process-exit boundary is explicit and caller-owned. Spring deliberately separates 'compute the exit code' (SpringApplication.exit() returns an int) from 'exit the process' (the caller does System.exit(code)). leaf must preserve this: the framework computes + publishes the code and RETURNS it (or returns a std::process::ExitCode via Termination from main); it NEVER calls std::process::exit itself inside the framework. This keeps the macro thin (§2.10 — #[leaf::main] emits a visible main returning a Termination / a std::process::exit tail nothing is hidden behind), lets embedded/test/library hosts observe the code without process death, and composes with Rust's idiomatic `fn main() -> ExitCode`.
- No reflective `instanceof ExitCodeGenerator` scan. Spring collects ExitCodeGenerator beans and ExitCodeExceptionMapper beans by runtime type-walk over the context. Rust has no instanceof / runtime type-scan: exit-code contributors that are BEANS are collected from the live container like any role, and contributors/mappers contributed at link time are origin-agnostic role DESCRIPTORS from a scoped catalog (Phase-2-undecided linkme/codegen/explicit), addressed by role — never discovered by inspecting live instances.
- Exit-code-from-exception cannot use reflective generic cause-matching. Spring's ExitCodeExceptionMapper + exceptions implementing ExitCodeGenerator rely on the JVM exception type hierarchy + reflection. leaf must map a failure to a code over its rust-native error model — a downcast over a typed error enum, an Any-based match, or a pattern-matched cause-chain — and since the unified error/diagnostics model is a deferred §3 keystone, the mapping mechanism (and the ExceptionExitMapper signature) must stay a CANDIDATE, not bake in a specific error representation. R3's bet (exit-code is a method on the typed RunFailure) is the tightest coupling to that deferred model.
